### 应对丧亲之痛与创业心法 (Coping with Grief and Entrepreneurship Lessons)

**Alex**: 我做了一次价值1.06亿美元的发布会。我妈妈有幸看到了，这真的很酷。但四周后她就去世了。当时的感觉就是，我现在该如何面对这一切？所以，我把这些推文当作写给自己的备忘录。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: I do a $106 million launch. My mom got to see it, which is really cool. She dies four weeks later. It was just like, how am I supposed to show up right now? So, I write these tweets as notes to self.

</details>

**Host**: 我以前从未听过你这样公开谈论。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: I'd never heard you talk like that publicly before.

</details>

**Alex**: 是的。我当时在想，我现在需要听到什么样的话？然后我想，我就把这些写下来吧，意思就是我必须继续战斗。人们会通过你选择承受多少痛苦来评判你对一个人的爱有多深。但我认为，你失去的那个人很可能并不希望你受苦。我从创业视角学到并应用到生活中的一个教训是，我们会遇到这些不满或不适的时刻，然后觉得“生活糟透了，我需要改变些什么”。但我的情感不适不足以成为改变我现在所做之事的充分理由。因此，要在商业上取得成功，仍然需要所有其他的技能。如果我要将14年的商业建议浓缩起来，我要说的第一件事就是，你必须决定，你是更在乎自己的未来，还是更在乎别人对你未来的看法。就像别人眼中那个版本的你必须得死，因为在创业的最初期，一切都归结于一件事，那就是恐惧。感到害怕是顺理成章的，因为我们渴望确定性，但知道答案的唯一方法就是开始行动。我学到的第二件事是，建立一家价值1000万美元公司的最快方法，并不是建立一家价值1亿美元公司的最快方法。因为专注和耐心是两项持久的竞争优势，因为它们是如此反人类。这就是企业家们会一飞冲天的地方。我稍后会详细解释这个概念。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: Yeah. I'm like, what do I need to hear right now? And I was like, I'll write that, which is like I just have to keep fighting. And people will judge how much you love someone by how much you choose to suffer. I don't think the person that you lost probably wants you to suffer. One of the lessons that I've learned from an entrepreneurship perspective translated into life is we will get these moments of dissatisfaction or discomfort and be like I'm life sucks. I need to change something. But my emotional discomfort is not an adequate reason to change what I'm doing. And so there's all these other skills that are still required to be successful in business. But if I can compress 14 years of business advice, the first thing I would say is you have to decide whether you care more about your future than what other people think about your future. Like someone's version of you has to die because everything in the very beginning of entrepreneurship comes down to one thing which is fear. And it makes sense to be afraid and it's because we want certainty, but the only way to know is to start. The second thing I've learned is that the fastest way to build a $10 million business is not the fastest way to build a $100 million business because focus and patience are the two enduring competitive advantages because they're so antihuman. And so this is where entrepreneurs will skyrocket. And I'll unpack that concept.

</details>

**Host**: 那么，你是如何看待你的业务以及AI（人工智能）这个世界的？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: So how are you thinking about your business, the world of AI?

</details>

**Alex**: 我是AI的坚定倡导者，但人们在错误的地方使用AI。比如，将思考和决策外包给AI是一个非常糟糕的决定，因为这只会让你变得更蠢。这是我目前拥有的最好的资产（指大脑）。所以我想要尽可能保持它的敏锐。最后，这可能是我能给出的最纯粹的真相，那就是我做过的最棒的财务决定。那就是……[轻哼]

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: I'm a big AI advocate, but people are using AI in the wrong places. like outsourcing thinking and decision-making to AI is a really bad decision because you will just get dumber. This is the best asset I've got for now. So, I want to keep it as sharp as I can. Finally, this is like the the absolute raw truth I could possibly give, which is the single best financial decision that I ever made. And that [snorts] is

</details>

### 频道的订阅呼吁 (Channel Subscription Appeal)

**Host**: 这对我来说非常有趣。我的团队给了我这份报告，向我展示了观看这个节目的你们中有多少人订阅了。根据这份报告，你们中的一些人告诉我们，你们不知怎么地就随机取消订阅了这个频道。所以，我想请大家帮个忙，如果您是本节目的常规观众并且喜欢我们在这里做的事情，现在请检查一下您是否按下了订阅按钮。就我们的订阅人数而言，这个节目正在接近一个相当重要的里程碑。所以，如果有一件简单且免费的事情您可以做来帮助我们、帮助我的团队以及这里的所有人，让这个节目保持免费，让它年复一年、周复一周地不断改进，那就是按下那个订阅按钮，并仔细检查一下您是否已经按过了。这是我唯一会要求你们做的事，我们说定了吗？如果你这样做了，我会告诉你我会怎么做。我保证每一周、每一个月，我们都会越来越努力，为你带来你想听到的嘉宾和对话。自《The Diary of a CEO》开播之初，我就一直信守着这个承诺，我绝不会让你们失望。请帮助我们，真的非常感谢。让我们继续节目。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: this is super interesting to me. My team given me this report to show me how many of you that watch this show subscribe. And some of you have told us according to this that you are unsubscribed from the channel randomly. So, favor to ask all of you, please could you check right now if you've hit the subscribe button, if you are a regular viewer of the show and you like what we do here. We're approaching quite a significant landmark on this show in terms of a subscriber number. So, if there was one simple free thing that you could do to help us, my team, everyone here to keep this show free, to keep it improving year over year and week over week, it is just to hit that subscribe button and to double check if you've hit it. Only thing I'll ever ask of you, do we have a deal? If you do it, I'll tell you what I'll do. I'll make sure every single week, every single month, we fight harder and harder and harder and harder to bring you the guests and conversations that you want to hear. I've stayed true to that promise since the very beginning of The Diary of a CEO and I will not let you down. Please help us. Really appreciate it. Let's get on with the show.

</details>

### AI如何改变商业与工作的本质 (How AI Changes Business and Work)

**Host**: Alex，我觉得我们必须从AI聊起，因为我认为这不仅是我的观众，也是我与处于创业各个阶段的创业者们交流时最关注的话题。前几天我在纽约和Uber的首席执行官Dara在一起，他谈论的就是AI；然后我与那些试图建立软件公司、却找不到可防御护城河的初创公司创始人交谈，那些对话也是关于AI的。作为一名企业家，你是如何应对这一点的？我们怎样才能利用AI更好、更快地完成那些属于商业常规职能的事情呢？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: Alex, I I feel like we have to start with AI because it is front of mind for both I think my audience but also for entrepreneurs that I speak to at every step in the journey whether they are I was with Dara who's the CEO of Uber the other day um in New York and he that's what the conversation was about AI but then I speak to startup founders who are trying to build a software company and can't find a moat that is defensible those conversations are about AI. What is your how are you approaching this as an entrepreneur? How can we use AI to do the things that are the art the normal functions of the business at a way better, way faster, etc.

</details>

**Alex**: 我看到很多现象，尤其是创始人或者刚起步的人，他们正在利用AI非常快地做很多愚蠢的事情，并且他们正试图建立一些无论如何都会被模型迅速吞噬的公司，或者这些模型对于超级用户而言已经做到了这一点，而且一切只会变得越来越简单。我想问那些现在正在“AI拉满”（AI maxing）的创业者们的问题是——我再说一遍，我认为采用新技术是非常好的——你现在赚到更多钱了吗？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: What I'm seeing a lot especially with founders or people are starting out is they're using AI to do a lot of dumb things really fast and they're trying to build companies that the models are going to swallow up pretty much immediately anyways or already have for super users and it's just going to like become easier. And the question that I would ask for the entrepreneurs who are you know AI maxing right now and again I think it's very good to adopt new technology is are you making more money

</details>

**Host**: 只是一个非常简单的问题，你现在赚到更多钱了吗？尽管你现在把token用到了极致。[笑声]

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: just the very simple are you making more money now and even though you're token maxing [laughter]

</details>

**Alex**: 然后花掉了所有这些token，那么你赚的钱变多了吗？前几天我看了一家公司，他们有11个虚拟助理（VA），正在做一些我记不太清了，类似于数据清理的工作。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: and spending all this tokens like are you making more money and I had a a business I looked at the other day they had 11 VAs that were doing I can't remember some sort of like data cleaning work

</details>

**Host**: VA。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: VAs

</details>

**Alex**: 是的，虚拟助理（virtual assistants），也就是海外的低技能劳动力在做这份工作，我觉得他们为这11个VA支付的费用大约是每个月11000美元。他们做得很好，一切都很顺利。然后他们花了35万美元试图创建一个AI系统，以取代这11个虚拟助理正在做的事情。但这意味着他们花掉了这11个虚拟助理三年以上的成本去做这些事。而且这甚至都不是他们业务的瓶颈。这只是他们能够自动化的一个流程而已。不是说他们应该将其自动化，因为那并不是限制他们增长的原因。他们仍然需要更多的客户，那（效率）并不是关键所在。这只是他们所谓“流程”的一部分之类的，但那并不是限制条件。他们没有足够的需求。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: yeah virtual assistants so low-skilled labor overseas that was doing work and I think it was something in the neighborhood of like $11,000 a month is what they were paying for these 11 VAs. And they were doing a good job and it all worked. And they then spent $350,000 to try and create an AI system that would replace what these 11 virtual assistants were doing. But that means they spent three plus years of costs on what these 11 virtual assistants were doing. And it wasn't even the constraint of the business. It was just a process they could automate. Not that they should automate it because it's not the thing that's limiting their growth. And so they still need more customers and that wasn't the thing. This was like part of their their quote process or something, but that wasn't the limitation. They didn't have enough demand.

</details>

**Host**: 人们正在错误的地方使用AI。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: People are using AI in the wrong places.

</details>

**Alex**: 他们试图在不该创办AI企业的时候去创办AI企业。他们应该在自己的业务中使用AI。他们试图将自己包装成AI企业来做广告，而实际上你只需宣传你的客户所关心的事情，即他们问题的解决方案和结果。这些都没有改变。但因为你对它感到非常兴奋，你就认为你的客户也会对此感到兴奋。但大多数人……很多人其实害怕AI。所以这甚至不一定是个正面因素，而且它也没有让你赚更多的钱，这是最终的问题。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: They're trying to start AI businesses when they shouldn't start AI businesses. They should use AI in their business. They're trying to advertise as AI businesses when you should just advertise the thing that your customer cares about, which is the solution to their problem, the outcome. None of that's changed. But because you got really excited about it, you think your customers are going to be excited about it. But most people are a lot of people are afraid of AI. Um, so it's not necessarily even a positive and it's not making you more money, which is the final question.

</details>

**Host**: 当你听像埃隆·马斯克（Elon Musk）这样的人讲话时，他说未来工作将成为一种可选项。我和几位AI专家坐在这里聊过，然后他们大体向我解释了它是什么。从一个非常简化的角度来看，这台机器内部有神经网络，其中包含大量参数，基本上就像人类大脑那样的连接。当他们训练它时，做出正确决策的参数会得到强化，而做出错误决策的参数则基本被剔除了。再声明一下，我这是极度简化了。这些大脑，这些数字大脑正变得越来越大。嗯，根据这些领域专家告诉我的话，我几乎无法想象，在某个节点，它难道不能完成人们目前正在做的大量认知工作吗？我这里真正的问题是，在一个智力变得廉价的世界里，价值将何存？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: When you listen to someone like Elon Musk, he says that work will become optional in in the in the future. And I sat here with a couple of experts on AI. Then they kind of explained to me what it is. And from a very simplified perspective, this machine that has these neural nets in it, which have lots of parameters, basically connections like the human brain does. And when they train it, the parameters become reinforced to the the ones that made the right decision. And the ones that made the wrong decision are like basically cut out. Again, I'm simplifying this. These brains, these digital brains are getting bigger and bigger and bigger. Um, it's it's like almost inconceivable to me based on what they've said to me, these area experts, that at some point it isn't able to do a lot of the cognitive work that people are currently doing. My question really here is like where does the value remain in a world where intelligence is cheap

</details>

**Alex**: 且极其丰富，甚至可以说更高级；另外，在一个机器人技术也即将到来的世界里，价值到底在哪里？我认为是承担风险和责任（stakes）。举个例子，AI可以给出世界上所有的建议，但必须有人来为这些决定拍板负责。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: and abundant and um higher one could say and also in a world where robotics are coming over the horizon too like where is the value I think stakes for example AI can give all of the recommendations in the world but someone has to own the decision

</details>

**Host**: 所以，就目前而言——也许未来会有所不同，AI并不是任何国家的公民，它不纳税，因此必须要有一个人来承担责任。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: and so as of [clears throat] right now maybe it will in the future AI isn't isn't a citizen of any you know any country it doesn't pay taxes and so somebody has to be responsible

</details>

**Alex**: 这是一种责任/负债（liability）。是的。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: it's a liability Yeah.

</details>

**Host**: 是的。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: Yeah.

</details>

**Alex**: 另一方面也有上行空间。把这个带入不同领域的媒体视角来看，想象一下吉米（Jimmy）说Mr. Beast（油管顶流博主）做了一个视频，而且Mr. Beast要被AI取代了。他不会被AI取代的，因为如果他有一个标题说“500万美元”的视频，而那500万美元不是真的，兰博基尼也不是真的，那就没有风险/赌注存在了。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: And upside on on the flip side. And to take this into different domains from the media perspective, imagine Jimmy said Mr. Beast has a video and like Mr. Beast is going to replace by AI. He's not going to get replaced by AI because if he had a video that said $5 million and the $5 million wasn't real and the Lamborghini wasn't real, there'd be no stakes.

</details>

**Host**: 是的。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Host**: Yeah.

</details>

**Alex**: 就像国际象棋对吧，现在的受欢迎程度比近代历史上任何时候都要高。但机器人下棋是可以打败人类的。

<details>

<!-- chunk 2/21 -->

### 人类的赌注与风险承担

**Speaker A**: F1 也是如此。你还是会希望刘易斯·汉密尔顿（Lewis Hamilton）坐在赛车里。如果你把他从赛车里换出来，你就不看 F1 了，对吧？所以人类是需要有赌注（stakes）的。因此，这就涉及到了报酬这一面，即必须有人要承担责任。必须有人要拿资金去冒险。有人为代币付了钱。有人拥有这家有限责任公司（LLC）。有人拥有这些。因此，在判断力、承担风险以及随之而来的收益中，是存在价值的。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: the F1 as well. You'd still want Lewis Hamilton in the car. You take him out the car, you stop watching F1, right? So humans want stakes. And so there's the compensation side of it, which is that somebody's going to be responsible. Someone has to risk the money. Somebody paid for the token. Somebody owns the LLC. Someone is is owns this. And so there is value in the judgment and the assumption of risk and upside.

</details>

**Speaker B**: 嗯。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: Mhm.

</details>

**Speaker A**: 这种价值可以是金钱，或者仅仅是责任。从媒体的角度来看，我们仍然需要把这种“赌注”引入到节目中。当然，现在也会有虚构的世界，它们受到的冲击会大得多。但如果你想做一档真人秀节目，那“真实”的部分就必须存在。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: Which can be money or just the responsibility. From a media perspective, we still have to introduce stakes into the into the show. Now, of course, there's going to be the fiction world. They're going to be much harder hit. But if you're trying to do a reality TV show, the real part has to be there.

</details>

### AI 时代的独立思考与实践

**Speaker B**: 我觉得你身上一个非常有趣的特质，就是你花了很多时间在思考上。在一个人工智能的世界里，现在很多人都在把他们的思考外包给像 ChatGPT 或 Claude 这样的前沿模型。我们以前也聊过这个，但我们双方都认同的一点是：知道该问什么正确的问题、保持思路的清晰、拥有批判性思维以及做决策的能力，在 AI 的世界里将是至关重要的。所以我的问题是，你平时是如何产生好点子、培养好点子，并给自己留出思考问题的心理空间的？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: One of the um things that I find really interesting about you is you spend a lot of time thinking and in a world of artificial intelligence, a lot of people are now deferring their thinking to these frontier models like chatb or claim or whatever. And um we've spoken about this before, but one of the things we both agree is that knowing the right questions to ask, clarity of thought, critical thinking, making the decision is going to be one of the things in a world of AI that's critically important. So my question to you is like how are you having good ideas cultivating good ideas and giving your space your sort of mental space to be able to think about problems?

</details>

**Speaker A**: 嗯。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: Mhm.

</details>

**Speaker B**: 你的具体做法是什么？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: What's your practice?

</details>

**Speaker A**: 我会从两个不同的角度来回答这个问题。首先，我认为就目前而言，把思考外包给 AI 仍然是一个非常糟糕的决定。我觉得任何使用过一段时间 AI 的人都知道，你可以让它顺从你的任何观点。当涉及到做决策时，这是很可怕的。所以每次，我觉得它虽然已经有一点进步了，但我还是会给它一个非常显而易见的决策去做。我会说：“好，我们打开 Claude。我们打开 OpenAI。我们把它们全都打开，然后我把同一个问题问这三个模型，结果它们的回答完全是五花八门的。” 所以我就会觉得：“好吧，这还是得靠我自己的判断。” 对吧？其次，如果你真的把决策权委托给它，你会变笨的。现在有很多这方面的研究，我相信你的播客里肯定也有嘉宾谈论过这个。对我来说，毫无疑问，我的大脑是我目前拥有的最好资产，所以我希望尽可能保持它的敏锐。千万不要把你最艰难的工作、最艰难的思考过程委派出去，因为你会因此变得非常脆弱，而且衰退得很快。过去三四个月里，我脑海中涌现出的一个新想法——我一直在反复琢磨的——就是当你把眼光放长远时会发生什么。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: I'll I'll take this from two different angles. So first is I think outsourcing thinking to AI as of right now is still a really bad decision if you it's like I think anybody who's used AI for any period of time knows that you can get it to agree to anything. ### 重建地基与反人性的耐心

**Speaker B**: 即使在退出的过程中，如果这是一家追求速度的公司，你会处于一种“求求你千万别出事，一切都要好好的”的状态。谁也别离职，对吧，大客户千万别流失。你就像是屏住呼吸熬这 6 个月，心里祈祷着：[笑声]“老天保佑，顺顺利利过去吧。” 然后，哪怕业绩只下滑了一两周，当他们问：“嘿，为什么可计费收入下降了？” 你就会掩饰说：“呃，不，这是暂时的波动。这在我们的预期之内。难道我没给你们发更新后的预测报告吗？” [笑声] 诸如此类的破事。我用画图来解释可能会更容易理解。但如果你要建一栋 100 层的大楼，你必须挖得更深。你必须采用不同的材料来往上建。所以，建 100 层和建 1 层相比，有 100 个决定你是要做出不同选择的。所以，真正困难的地方在于，人们什么都想要。他们说，我想要拥有建一楼平房的速度，但等他们建完一层后，他们又说：“其实吧，我想要一栋 10 层的大楼。” 但是地基不对。所以这就是为什么创业者们会经历这种事。他们会迅速飙升到这里，然后就停滞不前了。

**Speaker A**: 所以，你会在底线上看到利润被压缩，这就是，你懂的，“不惜一切代价增长”成为一个问题的时候。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: And so, you're going to see that in the bottom line getting compressed and this is where, you know, growth at all costs becomes a problem.

</details>

**Speaker B**: 但对于这位创业者来说，他花时间去弄清楚如何留住全部的100位客户。现在，如果他碰上了你我的分销渠道，然后突然间我们说，太好了，现在我知道我可以给这东西带来一万名新客户，那么这就会变成一门年入十亿的生意，因为我们首先解决了最重要的问题，那就是收入留存下来了。所以当人们在匆忙中创业时，他们做出来的东西不够好，结果你扩张得非常快。这就是为什么懂营销和销售可能会很危险，因为你营销和销售的能力越强，你营收增长得就越快，但你会达到一个点，你会有一定的销售速度瓶颈，我每个月只能卖给100人、200人、500或者1000人，不管多少。而在那个时候，你只有不断去寻找更多的分销渠道，因为你的“公交车后门有漏洞”，一旦销售停止，生意就会崩溃。而且我想说，这种情况比另一种情况——也就是你实际上有一门极具用户粘性的生意——要常见得多。

<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: But for this entrepreneur, he spent the time to figure out how to get all 100 customers to stay. And at that point, you either have to always be seeking more distribution because you have a hole in the back of your bus, but the moment the sales stop, the business craters. And I would say that that is more common than the alternative, which is that you actually have a sticky business.

### 关于定价与利润率

**Speaker A**: 人们在街上遇到我跟我聊的另一件事，当他们达到百万营收的那个阶段时，他们会说，你知道吗，我的客户很喜欢我。他们不断回头，但我已经没有时间了。基本上就是这样。

</details>

**Speaker B**: 是的，这很有意思，因为很多时候如果我也遇到同样的情况，有人说，嘿，我的时间不够用了。我会问的第一个问题是，“你的利润率是多少？”如果他们的利润率非常微薄，他们就请不起帮手。那是症状，而不是根本原因。根本原因要么是他们的报价不对，也就是说他们提供的东西，价格和交付条件相比太低了。你付出的太多而索取的太少，（清嗓子）

### 创业的第一步与克服他人的评判

**Speaker A**: 我的建议是，如果你还没有自己的企业，那么每个人的第一步都是一样的，那就是你必须先注册一个实体，然后你需要一个银行账户来进行资金交易，并且你必须有一种收取现金的方式。当然，你可以做纯现金生意，但我得说现在大多数人都在使用信用卡。所以，你需要一个支付处理器、一个银行账户和一个实体。

<summary>Original English</summary>

### 选择的自由与代价

**Speaker A**: 老兄，这越来越……好吧，这太棒了。所以关于这点，我认为它是选择的自由。

但我认为人们往往停留在“自由”这一层。现在似乎有一种对独立性、保留选择权、让人生选择最大化的狂热迷恋。但是，生活中所有最美好的部分，都发生在你行使选择权并推开一扇门之后。但在这样做的同时，你也把其他本可以走进去的门留在了身后。所以，过上你想要的生活的秘诀在于做出承诺。但你无法得到你想要的所有人生。所以在某个时刻，你必须站在悬崖边上做出一场交易。你必须说，比起别的，我更想要这个。这意味着我知道自己得不到那个了，而我选择说我更想要这个。我认为这就是人们在十字路口停滞不前的原因。这才是耗费了绝大部分时间的东西。不仅是在进入商业领域之前，当你已经在做生意时，人们也会坐在十字路口思考：我想建立什么样的公司？什么样的产品？我想要做什么样的生意？我们就坐在那里，当我看到人们卡住时，几乎总是因为未做出的决定，因为未做出的决定可以永远拖延下去。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: dude this gets okay this is great. So I think about this it's the freedom to choose

but I think people stop at freedom. There's this almost fetishization of independence and keeping your options open and m and options maxing right for your life. ### 内容策略与风险连续体

**Speaker A**: 考虑到现在算法和人工智能领域所发生的这一切翻天覆地的变化，你在自己的内容策略上有没有做出过任何调整？

<summary>Original English</summary>

</details>

**Speaker B**: 有的，我现在只专注于去做那些只有我才能做得到的事情。[清嗓子]

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: Yeah, I'm focused on only doing things that only I can do. [clears throat]

</details>

**Speaker B**: 绝大多数人没有能力让成百上千位企业老板每个月都亲自飞过来相聚，而且这些老板全都是拥有百万美元甚至更高身价的企业家，专门跑来针对那些每年营收在300万、500万、1000万，或者是50万美金的真实商业难题寻求指导意见。大多数人并没有这样的资源。因此，我可以用一种现场直播、实时互动的形式来展现我的专业知识，而这种方式是别人根本无法做到的。我认为这种现场直播的形式是一种——我的意思是，也许在未来的人工智能时代，我们会有能够去进行现场直播的 AI 虚拟数字人，但至少在短期到中期的这段时间里，我认为真人现场依然是非常有优势的。我认为 IRL（在现实生活中，In Real Life）的形式，也就是确实有真实的人类在进行面对面的交谈是非常重要的，就像我们刚刚推出并在节目里聊到过的《扩展还是失败》（Scale or Fail）这档真人秀。那里面有活生生的企业家，而且还涉及到真实的赌注和利害关系 [笑声]。我认为这就是某种永远不会被轻易取代的东西。但是如果我们把所有的内容都放在一个连续体上来思考，好吧，让我们把这个连续体称为“风险连续体”（continuum risk）。当我说风险的时候，指的是一个消费者在消费这些内容并将其实际执行落地时所需要承担的风险。因此，如果我们把娱乐和教育这两个概念区分开来——娱乐型内容的存在意义就是为了被消费。纯粹被消费是娱乐的唯一目的。而教育型内容的目的则是为了去改变人们的行为。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: Most people can't get hundreds of business owners to fly out in person every month that are all million-dollar plus business owners to seek advice on real business problems at 3 million, 5 million, 10 million a year, 500k a year. Most people don't have that. And so I can demonstrate expertise in a live and interactive fashion, in a way that other people can't do. I think that live is something that I mean AI maybe in the future we'll have some AI avatars that can go live but at least in the short to medium term I think live is good. I think IRL as in like it's actually real people talking which is we talked about the scaler fail show that we just launched. It's real entrepreneurs and there's stakes [laughter] involved. I think that is something that is not going to be replaced. But if we think about content on this continuum all right let's make this continuum risk. When I say risk it's the risk that a consumer has in consuming the content and executing on it. And so if we differentiate entertainment and education is entertainment the point of the content is to be consumed. It's the only objective of entertainment. The point of education is to change behavior.

</details>

**Speaker B**: 对吧？所以这就构成了我们在此处的分野。现在来看，娱乐内容创作者将会受到比教育内容创作者大得多的冲击。而那些牵涉到真实利益和赌注的娱乐创作者，将能够通过持续专注于真实生活（IRL）、真实的赌注以及真实的故事来维护好他们自身的护城河。现在，在教育这一端的风险世界里，让我们放上一些风险和成本非常低廉的东西。举个例子。比如说，我们把美妆内容放在连续体的这一端。如果我有一个专门做美妆教程的 AI 数字人网红，我不认为其中的风险会高到哪里去。因为如果那个教程教得真的很好，我不觉得会有一个女孩子在看了视频之后，会拒绝去使用他们在教程里传授的化妆技巧。我觉得她去尝试的可能性相当高。如果你沿着风险连续体再稍微往上走一点，风险再高一点，比如说关于个人理财的内容。那么，凭据和信誉到底是什么？在美妆领域，你的信誉就是你呈现出的美感。而在个人理财领域，你的信誉就在于：你自己有可观的个人财产吗？对吧？那么，谁是个人理财领域排名第一的顶尖意见领袖呢？在我看来，我认为是戴夫·拉姆齐（Dave Ramsey）。而且他碰巧也是——虽然我不认为他会这么称呼自己，但我认为他绝对是个亿万富翁。而且他拥有一份极其庞大的事业，我认为他已经公开过，他创造了每年高达三亿美元的营收。当然，我也知道还有一些其他更年轻、新时代的博主。嗯，我觉得薇薇安·图（Vivian Tu）也是个很棒的个人理财博主。我觉得艾丽卡·库尔伯格（Erica Kullberg）也涉猎了这个领域，她是那位做法律内容普及的女孩。嗯，不管怎么说，我要表达的意思是，他们所有人本身都拥有着强大的现实信誉背书。相比之下，如果你只是突然跑出来在网上散布一些如何省钱的建议和诀窍，那是毫无信誉可言的。现实世界，你缺乏现实世界的支撑。你没有任何能够拿来为自己背书的真材实料。

### 价值公式解析

**Guest**: 是的。所以核心——每本书都有一个核心概念，然后它会分解如何运用这个概念等等。但是“价值公式”（value equation）归根结底是：结果是什么？有些结果比其他结果更有价值。如果我能帮你理发，相比于我能让你拥有完美的身材，完美身材的价值更高。如果我能让你成为亿万富翁，相比于拥有完美身材，成为亿万富翁的价值更高。对吧。所以我们有“结果”（outcome），然后我们有“感知的实现可能性”（perceived likelihood of achievement）。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

Yeah. So the the core so each of the books has one core concept and then it breaks down how to use it etc. But the value equation comes down to what's the outcome and some outcomes are worth more than others. ### 拥抱不变的事物 (Embracing What Doesn't Change)

**Speaker A**: ……这是我规划未来时真正依赖的支柱。

**Speaker B**: 请给大家提供一些背景信息，因为有些人可能从来没有听过杰夫（Jeff Bezos）谈论过这个话题，以及它意味着什么。

**Speaker A**: 一家企业能做的最聪明的事情之一，就是打造一家看起来规模更大的公司，而不需要真正像大公司那样去招募全职人员。但我们都面临的问题是，大多数公司内部并不具备所有的技能。所以，当我观察如今获得真正成功的企业时，他们在所有人身上呈现出的一致模式，就是他们行动有多迅速。他们引入新兴领域具备技能的专家，以保持自身的领先地位。即便是在我们公司，过去一年里，我们也一直在招募像 AI 原生战略、无代码构建以及产品工作流等领域的顶尖人才。而我们正是通过我们的长期合作伙伴 Fiverr Pro 找到这些人才的。他们的高级服务只向你展示经过严格审查的人才。因此，你始终能得到一份保障：任何你引入来协助你完成复杂项目的人，都具备所需的能力。

### Behaviorism and Incentives

**Speaker B**: 所以当你把自己看作是被训练的对象时，我们就能更好地预测人们是否会购买，人们是否会接受这个提议。比方说，我有一位爷爷，他不想吃药，而医生说这药能让他延寿十年。好，护士和我在家，她打算试着给爷爷喂这颗药片。爷爷说：“这药的味道就像……我不想吃。”他直接把她推开了。护士退了回来，摊开双手说：“你可以把马牵到水边，但你不能强迫它喝水。”对吧？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: and so when you see yourself as the subject that is being trained then we can make better predictions of will people buy will people take this offer let's say I've got a grandpa and he doesn't want to take his medicine and the medicine the doctor said was going to extend his life by 10 years okay the nurse is at home with me and she's going to try and give grandpa the the pill. And he says, "It tastes like I don't want to have it." He just brushes her away. She comes back, she throws her hands up and she's like, "You lead a horse to water, you can't make it drink." Right?

</details>

**Speaker B**: 实用主义者或行为主义者会说：“不，是你让其他许多选项变成了比吃药更有可能的选项。”因此，我们需要做的是，让吃药成为他能获得的最美好、最简单的选择。所以你要做的第一件事就是把药片碾碎。然后我会说：“好吧，我们把它放进一些柠檬水里。把它放进冰箱里，冰镇一下。”

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: The operationalist or the behaviorist would say, "No, you've made many other options the more likely option than taking the pill." And so what we need to do is make the pill the nicest and easiest option available to him. And so the first thing you would do is you'd crush the pill up. And then I would say, "Okay, let's put it in some lemonade. Put it in the in the fridge, make it ice cold.

</details>

**Speaker B**: 好了，我要拿一些咸味的花生出来，然后把它放在爷爷面前，对吧？然后我要拿出一个，你知道的，国际象棋棋盘或双陆棋棋盘出来，然后坐在他面前，把一切都摆好，然后说：“嘿，我知道你之前说你想玩双陆棋，而过去三个星期我都拒绝了。这是柠檬水。你想玩吗？如果你喝完柠檬水，我们就可以玩。”现在爷爷喝柠檬水的可能性有多大？

### 改变人类行为的激励机制 (Incentive Structures and Behavioral Change)

**Speaker A**: 我们在劝说别人采取行动时，会使用各种说服要素。但我们也必须使用“反向操作”，这就好比在说：“嘿，另外我们还有这样的情况，假设你不报名参加我的健身项目。这就意味着，未来你将无法和孩子们共度这些美好时光，对吧？”好事减少了，坏事增加了，对吧？你会有更多的健康问题，更多的麻烦事。

### 定价作为服务价值与质量的强信号

**Speaker A**: 如果你看看一项服务的价格，这是专门针对服务型企业的，

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: If you look at the price of a service, and this is specifically for service businesses,

</details>

**Speaker B**: 服务的价格几乎与企业和企业主的高级程度有着一对一的关联……

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: the price of the service almost has a one to one correlation with how advanced the business and business owner are

</details>

**Speaker A**: 这仅仅是作为一种迫使功能，因为为了提高价格，就像一家做得好的服务型企业意味着你做了一份好工作。你的需求大于你的供应。所以当供需曲线交叉时你该怎么办？你就会提高价格。所以你继续这个循环。只要你继续提供比你收费更多的价值，你就会继续拥有更多的需求。然后你就会不断地提升。这就是为什么价格是衡量价值的一个如此强烈的信号。这也解释了为什么当你刚起步时，很难听进去我们说：“嘿，在你的价格标签上加个零。在你的价格标签上加两个零。”因为如果有人来找我，说：“嘿，我想以每月2,000美元的价格为你做所有的社交媒体管理。”我就会想，“老兄，你根本应付不了我。”

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker A**: just as a forcing function because in order to go up in price, like a service business done well means you did a good job. You have more demand than you have supply. And so what do you do when supply and demand crisscross? You go up in price. And so you continue this loop. And as long as you continue to deliver more value than you're charging for, you continue to have more demand. And you just keep lading up. And this is why price is such a strong signal for value. Which why when you're starting out, it's hard to hear us say, "Hey, add a zero to your price tag. Add two zeros to your price tag." Because if someone comes to me and says, "Hey, I want to do all of your social media management for $2,000 a month." I'm like, "Dude, you can't even handle me."

</details>

**Speaker B**: 就像你可能，就像即使我答应了，你也做不到。算不过账来。你根本不可能做到。所以如果有人来找我，我可能愿意花费的最低金额是，我不知道，每月15,000美元请一个供应商。就像我真正可能接受的最低金额，而且那可能是一家规模较小的店，我知道创始人可能仍然全面参与其中。我对此没意见，因为我没意见，有些创始人比如很优秀，基本上我把他们看作是我以较低薪水聘请的兼职员工，那也没问题。说了这么多，就是想说你想要提高你的价格。一是因为你服务于更好的客户，二是因为你有更好的利润率。三是因为会有更少的麻烦，四是因为这是你在你所做的事情上有多优秀的信号。而且最优秀的企业和企业主会知道，哦，这是适合我的。你向他们发出信号，表明你想要走向市场，但你的市场价格很低。这里，嗯，有一种叫做范韦斯滕多普（Van Westendorp）价格敏感度分析法的东西。你可能听说过。它源自20世纪70年代。我在推出新产品时经常会使用这个。所以你会问四个问题。第一，在什么价格下，这个产品会昂贵到你甚至都不会考虑它？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: Like you could like even if I said yes, you can't. it. The math doesn't math. There's no way you can do it. And so if someone comes to me like the minimum I'm probably going to be willing to spend is I don't know 15,000 a month for a vendor. ### 范韦斯滕多普价格敏感度分析与市场细分定价

### 用金钱奖励内部推荐的威力

**Speaker B**: 这也意味着，你可能对你的客户有持续的营销动作。但你觉得不应该对你的团队和员工有持续的营销。我们希望客户给我们推荐转介绍吗？是的。我们希望员工给我们推荐人选吗？我们有没有设立激励机制，让大家带来推荐？有一个很棒的故事。我朋友的朋友，经营着一家年收入大约 1000 万美元左右的企业。他和一个在同一领域、但已经走得远得多的人交谈。他所从事的业务有类似代理人的角色，大概就像是一家经纪公司。那位导师问他：“那么，你给你的代理人推荐其他代理人，提供了什么激励？”他说：“哦，你知道的，如果他们带进一个新的代理人，我会付给他们 500 美元。”导师问：“好吧，那你从一个高产的代理人身上每年能赚到多少毛利？”他回答是每年 25 万美元。一个高产的代理人，带来的毛利。导师说：“所以，你只愿意花 500 美元去赚 25 万美元？”导师又问：“那为了赚这 25 万美元，你愿意花多少钱？”他说：“这个嘛，我不知道，可能是 5 万美元吧。”导师说：“对啊，那为什么你不一开始就把推荐奖金设成 25000 美元呢？”于是，他把推荐奖金从 500 美元提高到了 25000 美元，然后他把公司的规模从 1000 万美元做到了 4 亿美元。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

>> which also means that you probably have ongoing marketing to your customers. You think there shouldn't be ongoing marketing to your team and employees. ### 目标与投入的博弈

**Alex**: 我需要知道我要去哪儿，也就是产出，以便掌控我的方向，但我日常关注的是我需要换挡，需要开车，需要给车加油。仅仅关注产出并不能让我们更快到达目的地，但我必须知道我的目的地。所以这又是我现在正在纠结的事情。说实话，我想不明白，而孩子随时都会到来[笑声]。他可不会等我想明白这一切，而且我很确信，也许有一天他看到这个播客时会说：“天哪，爸爸。真是的。”嗯，

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex**: need to know where I'm going the output in order to direct my car but what I'm focusing on a day-to-day is I need to shift gears I need to drive the car I need to fill it up with gas me focusing output is not going to get us there any faster but I have to know where I'm going and so this is again what I'm struggling with now to be fair I cannot figure it out and the kids can come either way [laughter] he's not going to wait for me to to figure this out and I'm sure maybe someday he'll look at this podcast and be like my god, dad. Jesus. Um,

</details>

**Speaker B**: 你知道有趣的是什么吗？我觉得汽车的比喻可能不太贴切，因为我当时也试图找另一个比喻，我偶然想到的一个是，在家里种一盆植物。你把种子埋进土里，你可能会想：“我真希望它能长到六英尺高，我真希望它能长出100片叶子”，但是你[笑声]能做的就只是给它浇水，给它阳光，它最终长成什么样就是什么样。

**Alex**: 我可能每天会思考好几次死亡。而且我认为这实际上与主观幸福感正相关。我的意思是，老年人无比清楚自己将会死去这个事实。我认为这就是为什么他们能……你可能看过这样一张图表，它展示了人们在人生的不同阶段对主观幸福感的评分。它是一个微笑曲线，我觉得这简直太有趣了。孩子们非常快乐，然后他们慢慢承担起越来越多的责任。而你最不快乐的巅峰时期大概是在45岁到55岁之间。那就像是个压力挤压期。你要照顾孩子，你要照顾父母，你还有事业。平心而论，在某些方面这是件好事，因为你正处于收入巅峰。在这段时间里，大多数人赚到了人生中最多的钱，但他们也不得不去面对这一切。他们承受着巨大的压力，对吧？他们被压力拉扯得很崩溃，然后随着年龄进入60岁、65岁、70岁，当我审视这两端——孩子的一端和老人的一端——时，老人其实在很多方面就像孩子一样，如果你想想看，他们依赖别人，他们主要就是在玩游戏[笑声]。在这两种情况下，他们都几乎没有什么责任，他们也不在乎别人的看法。如果你看到一个蹒跚学步的小孩穿着芭蕾舞裙和牛仔靴出门，那是因为他们根本不在乎别人怎么想。而当你走到生命尽头时，你的想法是：“老兄，反正我都要死了。”所以我一直非常痴迷于一件事，这件事有些极端但可能很准确，那就是我试图去想，等我80岁的时候我会如何看待这个世界？我怎样才能尽快在当下就达到那种境界？因为比起80岁时的我，现在的我更在乎别人的看法。我只想尽可能多地把那种状态提前，因为当我看到那些80岁的老人时，我想：“那家伙根本不在乎别人的看法了。”然后我就觉得，我非常向往那样。所以我写了很多关于死亡的文字，因为我觉得……这些推文算是写给自己的备忘录，不是在说教。当我写下这些东西的时候，它们是我对自己的心理自助。回想25年2月我写下那段话的时候，没错，2025年2月。25年第一季度是我个人过去八年来度过的最艰难的一个季度。

### 应对人生低谷与建立心理韧性

**受访者 (Interviewee)**: 著名的投资人比尔·阿克曼（Bill Ackman）曾经接受过一个非常精彩且发人深省的采访，我恰好机缘巧合下看到了其中的一个片段。在那个特定的时期，他刚刚经历了一场巨大的财务浩劫，损失了大概十亿美元之巨。不仅如此，他当时的个人生活也处于水深火热之中，正在经历离婚的煎熬，似乎同时还有另外一件同样糟糕且令人头疼的事情。我记得，大概是因为那巨大的十亿美元损失而卷入了一系列极其复杂的法律诉讼之类的麻烦，不管具体细节是什么吧。他在采访中说到，当你正处于那种极其艰难的时期，当你深陷那些人生低谷和泥沼无法自拔的时候，他所能做的就是全神贯注于唯一一件自己当下真正有能力掌控和解决的事情。他当时的切身感受是，面对如此庞大且错综复杂的困局，你不可能在短短一天之内就解决所有问题，你根本无法像完成一个简单任务那样立刻结束这一切噩梦。我认为，这就是这种困境最令人感到窒息和痛苦的地方——它就像是一个永远无法闭合的死循环，每当生活中的什么琐事或者念头让你重新回想起它时，你就仿佛再次跌入了这个不断反刍、反复自我折磨的深渊，你会控制不住地去想“如果事情变得更糟、彻底失控了该怎么办”，并不断地在脑海中把情况灾难化。他当时的应对方法非常朴素但有效：“面对这头巨大的大象，你只能一口一口地吃掉它，每次只集中精力解决眼前的一点点小问题，然后每熬过一两个月，你就可以回头审视一下走过的路，然后欣慰地对自己说，你看，我毕竟还是取得了一些实质性的进展。”他就是用这种心态，完全且纯粹地专注于取得哪怕是微小的进展，一点一滴地凿开并解决这个巨大的难题。我觉得，我当时所采取的应对策略差不多也是这种类似的方法，这有点像那种“只看眼前两步路，不去看遥远山峰”的务实策略——我会问自己，我现在实际真正能着手去做的事情到底是什么？那好，我就去把这些眼前能做好的事情做好。有时候，我还清楚地记得在那段极其难熬的时期里，我脑海中反复思考的很多东西都是关于这个的……所以我写下了一些文字，就在今年我母亲不幸过世的时候，我专门为自己写了一篇长文章。是的。嗯，我为自己写了一篇深入探讨“心理韧性”（mental toughness）的文章。我之所以写它，是因为我想要弄清楚——当然，这并不是说在你母亲刚刚去世这种悲痛时刻，你还非要强迫自己表现得多么冷血和坚强，这绝对不是我要表达的重点。我的重点在于：在这个彻底令人崩溃的当下，我到底应该以怎样的一种精神状态和行动姿态去继续面对生活？这差不多就是我当时内心真实的思考和挣扎过程。所以，我基本上是这样理性地看待这个问题的，那就是，当重大的负面事件、当所谓的“坏事”不可避免地发生时，对吧？我们可以把随之而来的各种反应和结果，看作是在几个不同维度上延伸的矢量。所以，你拥有的第一个维度是：需要持续多长时间的负面影响，或者说需要发生多少件糟糕透顶的事情，才会最终导致你改变自己原有的行为模式。让我们在脑海中想象一下，这条平稳的基准线代表了我们日常的行事方式和性格状态。到底要发生多少件坏事，我们才会在压力下屈服并改变我们的行为方式？我们会不会因此变得对配偶更加缺乏耐心、更加易怒？我们会不会在工作岗位上变得心不在焉、频频出错？就像是，我们要受到多少次打击？如果假设存在一个拥有完美、不可摧毁的心理韧性的人，他或许能够默默承受无限多的灾难性事件降临在自己头上，而他的行为方式、他的道德准则和日常运转依然能够坚如磐石、保持不变——当然，这仅仅是从纯粹的理论角度来看待这个问题。那么接下来的一个更为现实的问题就是，好吧，假设你和我一样，并不是一个心理绝对完美、无坚不摧的人，当有些糟糕的事情不断累积，最终突破了你的忍耐极限和心理防御阈值，导致你的行为不可避免地发生了改变，那么我们就会面临下一个需要衡量的维度。所以我们有了这样的评估标准：你能在这个重压下坚持多久，你究竟能承受多大的打击？当我们最终支撑不住摔倒时，我们会跌入多深的谷底？你的行为究竟会恶化到什么程度？你仅仅是会变得对你的员工大发雷霆、失去耐心，还是会彻底自暴自弃，甚至去沾染海洛因这种毒品？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Interviewee**: and Bill Aman had this really great interview where there's a snippet that I ended up seeing which was he was he had just lost like a billion dollars. he was getting divorced and there was like one other thing. I think there was lawsuits because of the billion dollars, whatever. And and he said when you're going through that that those rough patches, he said he focused on the only thing he could do when he's like you can't you're not going to solve this in a day. Like it you can't finish it. And I think that's what that's what's so painful about it is you have this open loop that whenever it whenever something reminds you of it, it's like you get into this loop again of like rumination and just like a what if it goes wrong and catastrophizing. And he's like, "You just have to take one bite at a time and then every, you know, month or two months, you can look back and be like, I made some progress." And he's like, he just focused on making progress and chipping away at the problem. And I think that was more or less the approach I had, which is kind of like the the two feet in front of you, like what are the things that I can actually do right now? I will do those. And sometimes, and I remember in that season, a lot of what I was thinking about was so I wrote, so when my mother passed uh this year, I wrote an article for myself. Yeah. Um, I wrote an article for myself about mental toughness and it was because I wanted to, of course, it's not like be mentally tough when your mother dies. That's not the point. It was like, how am I supposed to show up right now? That was kind of like the the thought process I had. And so I basically here this so I saw it like this, which is bad thing happens, right? There's these vectors of what happens as a result. So you have how long or how many bad things have to happen before you change your behavior. So let's imagine this line is how we're acting. How many bad things have to happen in order for us to change how we act? Are we shorter with our spouse? Do we not pay attention at work? Like what how many bad things if somebody the perfect perfectly mentally tough person would be able to have an unlimited amount of things happen to them and then their behavior would remain the same from purely from that perspective. The next issue is okay well let's say that you're not a perfectly mentally tough person and something crosses your tolerance threshold and then your behavior changes and so we have this next measurement. So we have how long and how much can you handle? We have how deep do you go when you fall? How how badly do you change your behavior? Do you just are you short with your staff or do you get into heroin?

</details>

**采访者 (Interviewer)**: 不是的，就像是，情况到底会有多糟？当你跌倒时，你会跌落到多么惨烈、多么糟糕的境地？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Interviewer**: No, like how bad how badly do you fall?

</details>

**受访者 (Interviewee)**: 然后紧接着的下一部分，也就是另一个衡量维度是：你需要花费多长的时间才能够从谷底爬起来、慢慢恢复，并最终重新回到你原本正常的心理机能和工作状态？然后，我自己在这个理论模型之上又补充了非常关键的一点，那就是：当我终于费尽力气恢复到原本的正常机能状态时，我是否因为这场磨难而变得比以前更好了？我是仅仅恢复到了和以前一模一样的水平，还是说，我已经遭受了不可逆转的永久性创伤，变得比以前更糟了？

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Interviewee**: And then the next piece is how long does it take you to recover, return back to original function? ### 面对失去与反思痛苦

**受访者 (Interviewee)**: 因此，通过清晰地描绘出这四个行为反应维度的变化轨迹，我得以在那个令我痛不欲生、极其艰难的人生季节里，重新审视和剖析自己。而这真是一件让人感觉十分荒谬、甚至充满着命运讽刺意味的事情，因为你仔细想想看。我刚刚策划并完成了一次价值高达1.06亿美元的、史无前例的超大规模产品发布活动。我的母亲在世时亲眼见证了这一切的发生，这对我来说真的是一件非常酷、非常有意义的事情。然而，仅仅在四周之后，她就永远地离开了人世。我实现了我长久以来为之奋斗的目标，我们总是在商业上谈论长期目标，对吧？一亿美元，一亿美元，一亿美元。出版那些书的最终目标，就是要在那一刻实现一亿美元的惊人收益。《一亿美元Offer》这本书本身所提供的提案，好到足以让人们觉得不买简直就是无可救药的傻子。我把这本书几乎是免费地送了出去，或者说只象征性地卖了99美分，而且我还附送了一整套高价值的配套课程。在此之前，这个行业里从来没有任何人敢这么干过。所以，正是因为这是一个如此不可思议、好到令人难以拒绝的Offer，我们获得了海量的潜在优质客户。我进行了一次规模巨大、简直如同天文数字般庞大的发布活动，但我并没有借此机会去直接收割变现，因为我内心深处只是想向所有人展示，当你对某件极具价值的产品进行那种疯狂的、铺天盖地的广告宣传时，究竟能够产生怎样震撼市场的惊人效果。而且我完完全全地运用了书里所写的所有营销策略来宣传这本书本身。要知道，这本书的主题恰恰就是关于如何有效变现的。因此，我手握一个极具吸引力且令人无法拒绝的完美Offer、配合着疯狂的广告宣传攻势，以及隐藏在其背后的一套严密且高效的商业赚钱模式，这就是为什么我能够在一个短短的周末就狂揽一亿美元的根本原因。

### “奋斗者”的心态与幸福的基线

**Speaker B**: 大概是吧。阿瑟·布鲁克斯（Arthur Brooks）的一些观点很有启发，你知道他的理论。他专门谈论了那些“奋斗者”，我当时的反应是，别跟我说这个了。[笑声] 他把奋斗者定义为像你这样的人。他们是高成就者。非常有趣的是，他描述了奋斗者典型的童年。他说的几乎分毫不差。就是这样的。他说，通常是移民父母，或者类似的情况，他们希望孩子表现出色，并且只有在孩子做了好事情时才给予认可。然后发生的事情是，孩子学到了爱是需要赚取的，于是你就学会了这个循环。你在这个循环中被不断强化。然后你赢得了更多，赚取了更多的爱。接着你步入广阔的世界，然后你会想，我该如何获得更多这种“毒品”，这种“爱的毒品”，这意味着我需要尽可能多地赚取？我需要尽可能多地赚取。所以你就对工作的循环上瘾了。我觉得这非常引人深思。但他的研究中还有另一部分，实际上在某种程度上让我感到非常踏实，那就是大约一半的主观幸福感是基因决定的。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Speaker B**: Probably. One of the things that Arthur Brooks has been really helpful you know his stuff. Um and he talks specifically to strivers and I'm like stop talking to me. [laughter] So strivers he defines as people who like you are strivers. They're high achievers. And what's really interesting is he describes what the childhood of a strive is. He's like almost to a tea. This is what it is. He's like, it's typically an immigrant parent or something like that where they're trying to do a good job and they basically withhold approval only when the kid does a good thing. And then what happens is the kid learns that love is earned and then you learn that loop. You're reinforced for that loop. And then you do win more and you earn more love. And then you get into the big wide world and then you're like, how do I get more of this drug, this love drug, which means I need to earn as much as possible? I need to earn as much as possible. So you just get addicted to the loop of working. And so I find this really fascinating. ### 应对生活与商业中的情绪波动

**Alex Hormozi**: 无论是有阴雨天，还是有梅雨季，抑或是有晴朗的季节，我们都会经历这一切。即使情况有所好转，我们的基准线也会随之调整。因为如果我们把一年作为一个整体来考虑——实际上我也向我的销售团队举过这个例子。如果我们把这看作是一年，并且我们有一年中的所有日子，那么一半的日子会高于平均水平，而另一半则会低于平均水平。每个月你都会有三次处于后10%的糟糕日子，因为一年中有36天会毫无理由地成为后10%的日子，这纯粹是大数定律在起作用。同时，你每个月也会有三天——几乎是每周一天——会成为全年排名前10%的好日子。这相当可观，因为几乎每周就有一天。所以，我经常会思考这种波动性。当我度过一个所谓的“糟糕的一天”时——因为我也会有这样的日子，而且是经常有——我就只会想，这大概只是那后10%的日子之一罢了。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex Hormozi**: there's rainy days and there's rainy seasons and there's sunny seasons and we go through them all. And even if things get better, our baseline adjusts so that yet again cuz like if we think about a year, I actually give this example to my sales team. So if we think about this as a year and we have all the days of the year, half the days are going to be above average and half the days are going to be below average. And every month you're going to have a bottom 10% day three times because you have 36 days per year that are going to be bottom 10% days for no reason purely because law of large numbers and you're also going to have three days per month which is almost one a week. That's going be a top 10% day of the whole year. Pretty impressive. But it's one a week almost. Right. And so I think about this variability a lot because when I'm having a quote bad day because I have them I have I have them all the time. Um and I just think this is probably just the bottom 10% day.

</details>

**Alex Hormozi**: 并且，我想我从创业视角转化到生活中的其中一个教训就是：我情绪上的不适，并不是改变我当前所做之事的充分理由。所以，我们会经历这些不满或不适的时刻，并且会觉得：“生活糟透了，我需要改变一些事情。”如果你对你的整体生活不满意，那就去改变一些东西。但如果你只是对今天的生活不满意，也许不要直接和你妻子分手。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex Hormozi**: And like and [clears throat] I think the the one of the one of the lessons that I've learned from an entrepreneurship perspective translated into life is my emotional discomfort is not an adequate reason to change what I'm doing. And so we will get these moments of dissatisfaction or discomfort and be like I'm life sucks. I need to change something. And if you don't like your life in general, change something. But if you don't like your life today, maybe don't break up with your wife.

</details>

**Alex Hormozi**: 也许可以先缓一天，对吧？所以我想，我再次从创业者的角度出发来看待这个问题，那就是人们在度过糟糕的一天后，往往会把情绪发泄在自己的生意上，然后改变一堆事情，最后毁掉了这个好不容易才起步的初创公司，仅仅是因为他们无法忍受某种情绪上的需求。这也是为什么这个图表对我来说如此有价值——我那处于后10%的糟糕日子，是否严重到足以让我改变自己的行为？如果我认定自己即使过了糟糕的一天也依然能做好工作，那这就是美好的一天。

<details>
<summary>Original English</summary>

**Alex Hormozi**: Maybe give it a day. Right? And so I think again, I come from the entrepreneur perspective, which is that people will have a bad day and then basically take it out on their business and then change a bunch of things and then destroy what they're with this fledgling business that's getting going because they had an emotional need that they could not tolerate. And so that's why this graph has been so valuable to me, which is is my bottom 10% day a big enough thing for me to change my behavior. If I determine that I can have a bad day and still do good work, that is a good day.

</details>
